Â Â Jessika Roswall, Member of the Commission. â Mr President, honourable Members, let me start by confirming this Commission's commitment to advancing gender equality and defending women's rights across the EU and beyond. We have expressed this commitment in the roadmap for women's rights adopted in March this year, ahead of International Women's Day. This roadmap, with its declaration of principles for a gender equalâsociety, is our compass to put women's rights and the gender perspective at the core of EU policies and actions.

We need strong unity between EU institutions in rallying behind this important declaration and its principles. It will strengthen the impact of our joint work on women's rights and speed up the progress toward gender equality. I would like to thank this House for its unwavering dedication and relentless efforts to protect and advance women's rights. I would like to express my gratitude to the chair and the rapporteur and all the members of the committee for advancing a resolution to endorse the roadmap for women's rights. Endorsement by the European Parliament will set a shared framework for action for the coming years.

Adoption of the new gender equality strategy is among key priorities for the coming years in the President's letter of intent, and this reflects its key role in fulfilling the objectives outlined in the roadmap. This oral question addressed to the Commission gives me the opportunity to talk about the next steps and the preparation of the new gender equality strategy 2026 to 2030.

We have carried out a comprehensive consultation process, gathering input from the public and a wide range of stakeholders. It includes an open public consultation and several meetings with stakeholders, social partners, equity bodies, international organisations, civil society organisations, as well as relevant EU agencies. We are now analysing this input. We are also eagerly awaiting the European Parliament's own initiative report on the future gender equality strategy, which I understand will be voted on in the next few weeks.

In drafting the future strategy, the European Parliament's input in its past positions on gender equality and in the upcoming report will be a fundamental contribution to our work. The new strategy will reflect ambitious actions based on the priority areas underlined in the President's political guidelines, the longâterm vision of the roadmap, stakeholder consultations and the data gathered and analysed during the preparatory work.

You also asked whether the strategy will provide for legislative action. It is important to emphasise that despite the extensive work that has already been done to implement the gender equality legislation adopted in recent years, our work on this must continue. Ensuring proper transposition and implementation in the Member States is essential to ensure that rights introduced or strengthened by the new directives do not exist only theoretically. They need to have a real effect for all EU citizens, and this does not preclude the possibility of new legislation, if needed, to reach the objectives of the roadmap for women's rights.

You have our commitment that we will carefully examine and consider all the proposals formulated by this House. We will join forces with you to work towards achieving the goals set out in the roadmap for women's rights. This is needed to deliver on the new gender equality strategy once it is adopted by the Commission. I thank you for the attention and am really looking forward to the debate.

Â Â Jessika Roswall, Member of the Commission. â Mr President, honourable Members, on behalf of the Commission, I would like to welcome Mr Beleris's report, which offers a comprehensive overview of the activities of the Committee on Petitions in 2024. Your committee has a special role in engaging with EU citizens; you listen to their concerns. For the EU institutions, it is vital to bring EU legislation and policy closer to people. Identifying and addressing citizen concerns was particularly important in the run-up to the European election in 2024. As your report rightly mentioned, the Commission remains the natural partner of the Committee on Petitions as the EU institution responsible for ensuring the implementation of and compliance with EU law. I welcome the continued constructive cooperation between our institutions.

The Commission has increased its efforts to provide timely and pertinent contributions to your responses to citizens. We are also endeavouring to offer your clear and simple observations that address the concerns of our citizens. When it comes to figures; according to your report, you sent us 377Â new petitions for our opinion. In total, the Commission provided opinions on 521Â petitions in 2024. These numbers testify the efforts and resources deployed by the Commission to ensure that all petitions you sent are being addressed.

We take notice that for the third year in a row, the environment remains the most frequent issue raised by petitioners, followed by personal matters, fundamental rights, justice and the internal market. Let me reassure you that the priorities of this Commission â as outlined in President von der Leyen's Political Guidelines in 2024 and reiterated in the State of the Union address last month â fully respond to the concerns raised by citizens. As regards environment, the Commission often focuses its enforcement action on zero pollution, circular economy and biodiversity. In addition, we prioritise enforcement and dialogue on environmental governance issues and these cross-cutting areas underpin the delivery of the European Green Deal's priorities.

I would like to reiterate our commitment on addressing petitions that raise concerns on the enforcement of EU law. The implementation of EU law is a core priority under this mandate and a joint endeavour of EU institutions and Member States. This is why we welcome your call for more active cooperation with Member States to unblock those petitions requiring prompt response and reactions from national authorities. When it comes to enforcement of EU law, the Commission acts when necessary using infringement procedures, but the infringement procedures are not meant to offer concrete solutions for individuals or ensure individual redress. Rather, it's aimed at addressing systematic problems affecting a significant proportion of the population across Member States. In individual cases where petitioners have complaints about the incorrect application of EU law, they would benefit more from the mechanisms available at national level, such as national courts, regulatory bodies or the ombudsman. If the problem has a cross-border dimension, the Solvit network may offer quick and flexible remedies.

Honourable Members, we have also listened to your calls for more transparency and better information sharing with regards to our enforcement actions. The Commission published its decisions for every step of infringement procedures on the EU's webpage. Moreover, since 2024, there has been a link on the infringement register to your petition portal so that citizens can search for cases linked to the petition. In April this year, the Commission launched a new user-friendly website offering up-to-date information on infringement procedures, transposition of directives, and pre-infringement dialogues. These tools help to track the progress of specific infringement cases and verify if there is any petition linked to ongoing investigations. Furthermore, the Commissioners will be in a position to inform the follow-up on petitions that allege a breach of EU law in their policy area when they present the Annual Progress Report on Simplification, Implementation and Enforcement to the responsible Parliamentary Committee.

Finally, the Commission commends your constructive cooperation with the European Ombudsman, with whom you share the objectives of ensuring the transparency, professionalism and integrity of the EU institutions vis-a-vis EU citizens.

5. World Mental Health Day - addressing the socio-economic factors (debate)
Video of the speeches

Â Â President. â The next item is the debate on the Commission statement on World Mental Health Day - addressing the socio-economic factors (2025/2907(RSP)).

Â Â Jessika Roswall, Member of the Commission. â MrÂ President, honourable Members, in recent years, we have seen poor mental health emerge as a significant public health issue. The COVID-19 pandemic especially has left a lasting mark on people's mental well-being, particularly on children and young people. According to the 'Health at a Glance: Europe 2022' report, 1 in 2 young Europeans reported unmet mental health needs, and the rates of depression among young people more than doubled during this period.

These days, more and more young people are using smartphones and accessing the internet. More and more are spending a lot of their time online. And, on the one hand, social platforms offer a space of connection, learning and creativity, but on the other hand, the digital environment is not always safe. Cyber bullying, privacy and safety threats, hate speech, harmful or inappropriate content, misinformation and addictive algorithms â these are just a few of the risks around being online, not to mention the physical impact that excessive screen time has.Â These things can cause compulsive and addictive behaviour, and they can make mental health issues worse in young people.

This is why we need the right safeguards. In her State of the Union speech this year, President von der Leyen emphasised the need to protect children from social media risks. She highlighted the importance of supporting parents and proposed exploring the need for age restrictions on social media. An expert panel is planned by the end of this year to advise on the best approaches for Europe to take. We are also preparing an EU-wide inquiry into the broader impacts of social media on young people's mental health and well-being.

Ahead of Mental Health Day tomorrow, we need everybody's commitment. If we want to make a difference, we need to work together with all public and private stakeholders, including industry and digital businesses. This is the only way we can ensure the highest safety and privacy standards.

In 2023, we adopted the communication on a comprehensive approach to mental health, which has 20 flagship initiatives and almost EURÂ 1.3Â billion in funding opportunities. It has a strong focus on children and young people. For example, one action is our work with UNICEF to develop a prevention toolkit to help improve the mental and physical health of children and teenagers and to understand how social media affects them.

We are also collecting best and most promising practices on mental health. With EURÂ 6Â million in support from the EU4Health programme, we are supporting Member States in transferring some of these practices across the EU. One example of this is a programme on community suicide prevention led by the Basque Health Service. Another example is the ABCs of Mental Health project. It aims to improve the mental health and well-being of disadvantaged groups across Europe, using a common research-based model that can beÂ adapted locally.

Honourable Members, the Commission is taking important steps to strengthen mental health support. For young people especially, this means looking at the impact of the digital world. New generations should be enjoying the benefits of technology, not suffering because of it.

The Commission looks forward to working with the European Parliament on this important issue.

11. Public health risks in a Europe affected by global warming: tackling the spread of mosquito- and tick-borne diseases (vote)
Video of the speeches
Â 

Â Â Presidente. â L'ordine del giorno reca la discussione sulla dichiarazione della Commissione sui rischi per la salute pubblica in un'Europa colpita dal riscaldamento globale: lotta alla diffusione delle malattie trasmesse da zecche e zanzare (2025/2910(RSP)).

Â 
Â Â 
Â 

Â Â Jessika Roswall, Member of the Commission. â Madam President, honourable Members â where the cold once kept certain insects at bay, data shows that warmer and wetter weather has led to an expansion of the mosquito and tick populations across Europe. Our changing climate is providing the conditions for infectious diseases like dengue, Lyme disease, West Nile virus, chikungunya, Zika and Crimean-Congo haemorrhagic fever to spread in Europe more recently.

One of the main carriers or vectors of these diseases is the tiger mosquito. This mosquito has expanded its territory significantly and can now be found in 13Â Member States. Europe has seen 27Â clusters of locally acquired chikungunya infections so far this year, which is a new record for our continent. And, for the first time, locally acquired chikungunya cases have been reported in France's Alsace region. This is a sign that the risk is spreading steadily. Therefore, we must treat these threats as a key public health concern. This is why the Commission, together with these agencies and in cooperation with Member States, is improving how we prevent, prepare for and respond to these diseases.

We also work closely with Member States and partner countries on the topic of vector-borne diseases through the Health Security Committee, and we have appointed three European reference laboratories covering vector-borne diseases. In turn, they support national reference laboratories in making testing and detection methods more consistent across Europe. We are running a pilot project on mosquito control techniques with the Cypriot public health authorities. We work closely with the European Centre for Disease Prevention and Control (ECDC) as it improves vector surveillance activities at country level, with tailored recommendations for countries that haven't faced these threats before. We invest in the research and development of new vaccines, anti-viral treatments and innovative diagnostic tests which encompass dengue, Zika and the West Nile virus, while also supporting clinical studies for vaccines.

But more is needed. This includes making health strategies more practical, holistic and forward-looking. On top of that, national approaches to climate adaptation should integrate more health considerations, particularly at local level. Closer cooperation between all stakeholders is necessary to strengthen these efforts. To support this and to strengthen Europe's overall resilience, work under the Preparedness Union Strategy is on its way. We will soon present the Union prevention, preparedness and response plan for health crises. The call for evidence is currently open for submission until 29Â October. The Union plan is expected to provide a practical overview of our arrangement for governance capacities and resources to respond to health crises, including on vector surveillance and control.

In July, we adopted the Medical Countermeasures Strategy, which identify vector-borne diseases as one of the top four threat categories. The EU needs to prepare for and outline related medical countermeasures. We will also develop a wastewater surveillance system that can detect outbreaks as they emerge, and we will provide financial support to boost Member States' vector-control systems. This work has already started because there is no time to lose â emerging vector-borne diseases, present a significant public health risk for Europe and working together gives us the best chance against them.

Â 
Â Â 
Â
